lang="en">Matter</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the concept in the physical sciences. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Matter_(disambiguation)" title="Matter (disambiguation)">Matter (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption>Matter</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quartz_oisan.jpg"><img alt="Quartz oisan.jpg" data-file-height="4031" data-file-width="4004" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Quartz_oisan.jpg/200px-Quartz_oisan.jpg" 2x" width="200"/></a><br/> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drop_closeup.jpg"><img alt="Drop closeup.jpg" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="839" decoding="async" height="203" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="444" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/NO2-N2O4.jpg/200px-NO2-N2O4.jpg" width="200"/></a><br/> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plasma-lamp_2.jpg"><img alt="Plasma-lamp 2.jpg" data-file-height="1609" data-file-width="1589" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Plasma-lamp_2.jpg/200px-Plasma-lamp_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Plasma-lamp_2.jpg/300px-Plasma-lamp_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Plasma-lamp_2.jpg/400px-Plasma-lamp_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">Matter is usually classified into <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter#The_four_fundamental_states" title="State of matter">three classical states</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> sometimes added as a fourth state. From top to bottom: <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>), <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> (<a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>), <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>), and a <a href="/wiki/Plasma_globe" title="Plasma globe">plasma globe</a> (<a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>).</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a> and general chemistry, <b>matter</b> is any substance that has <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> and takes up space by having <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penrose_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penrose-1">[1]</a></sup> All everyday objects that can be touched are ultimately composed of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, which are made up of interacting <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>, and in everyday as well as scientific usage, "matter" generally includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atoms" title="Atoms">atoms</a> and anything made up of them, and any particles (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_particle" title="Composite particle">combination of particles</a>) that act as if they have both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> and volume. However it does not include <a href="/wiki/Massless_particle" title="Massless particle">massless particles</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>, or other energy phenomena or waves such as <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penrose_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Penrose-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgrawhill_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcgrawhill-2">[2]</a></sup>  Matter exists in various <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">states</a> (also known as <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phases</a>). These include classical everyday phases such as <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> &#8211; for example <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> exists as ice, liquid water, and gaseous steam &#8211; but other states are possible, including <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein condensate">Bose&#8211;Einstein condensates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermionic_condensate" title="Fermionic condensate">fermionic condensates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Quark%E2%80%93gluon_plasma" title="Quark&#8211;gluon plasma">quark&#8211;gluon plasma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RHIC_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-RHIC-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Usually atoms can be imagined as a <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, and a surrounding "cloud" of orbiting <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> which "take up space".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hooft_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hooft-5">[5]</a></sup>  However this is only somewhat correct, because subatomic particles and their properties are governed by their <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum nature</a>, which means they do not act as everyday objects appear to act &#8211; they can act like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave-particle_duality" title="Wave-particle duality">waves as well as particles</a> and they do not have well-defined sizes or positions. In the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, matter is not a fundamental concept because the <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary constituents</a> of atoms are <a href="/wiki/Quantum" title="Quantum">quantum</a> entities which do not have an inherent "size" or "<a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a>" in any everyday sense of the word. Due to the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">exclusion principle</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interactions</a>, some "<a href="/wiki/Point_particle" title="Point particle">point particles</a>" known as <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a> (<a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>), and many composites and atoms, are effectively forced to keep a distance from other particles under everyday conditions; this creates the property of matter which appears to us as matter taking up space.\n</p><p>For much of the history of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural sciences</a> people have contemplated the exact nature of matter. The idea that matter was built of discrete building blocks, the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate_theory_of_matter" title="Particulate theory of matter">particulate theory of matter</a>, was first put forward by the Greek philosophers <a href="/wiki/Leucippus" title="Leucippus">Leucippus</a> (~490 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> (~470&#8211;380 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_mass">Comparison with mass</span></h2>\n<p>Matter should not be confused with mass, as the two are not the same in modern physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mongillo_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mongillo-7">[7]</a></sup> Matter is a general term describing any \'<b>physical substance\'</b>. By contrast,  <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> is not a substance but rather a quantitative <i>property</i> of matter and other substances or systems; various types of mass are defined within <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> &#8211; including but not limited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_mass" title="Inertial mass">inertial mass</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_mass" title="Relativistic mass">relativistic mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">mass&#8211;energy</a>. \n</p><p>While there are different views on what should be considered matter, the mass of a substance has exact scientific definitions.  Another difference is that matter has an "opposite" called antimatter, but mass has no opposite&#8212;there is no such thing as "anti-mass" or <a href="/wiki/Negative_mass" title="Negative mass">negative mass</a>, so far as is known, although scientists do discuss the concept.  Antimatter has the same (i.e. positive) mass property as its normal matter counterpart.\n</p><p>Different fields of science use the term matter in different, and sometimes incompatible, ways. Some of these ways are based on loose historical meanings, from a time when there was no reason to distinguish mass from simply a quantity of matter. As such, there is no single universally agreed scientific meaning of the word "matter". Scientifically, the term "mass" is well-defined, but "matter" can be defined in several ways. Sometimes in the field of physics "matter" is simply equated with particles that exhibit rest mass (i.e., that cannot travel at the speed of light), such as quarks and leptons. However, in both <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, matter exhibits both <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a>-like and <a href="/wiki/Particle" title="Particle">particle</a>-like properties, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Wave%E2%80%93particle_duality" title="Wave&#8211;particle duality">wave&#8211;particle duality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberg_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberg-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masujima_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masujima-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Based_on_atoms">Based on atoms</span></h3>\n<p>A definition of "matter" based on its physical and chemical structure is: <i>matter is made up of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barker0_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barker0-11">[11]</a></sup>  Such <i>atomic matter</i> is also sometimes termed <i>ordinary matter</i>. As an example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deoxyribonucleic_acid" title="Deoxyribonucleic acid">deoxyribonucleic acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> (DNA) are matter under this definition because they are made of atoms. This definition can be extended to include charged atoms and molecules, so as to include <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasmas</a> (gases of ions) and <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a> (ionic solutions), which are not obviously included in the atoms definition. Alternatively, one can adopt the <a href="#Protons,_neutrons_and_electrons_definition"><i>protons, neutrons, and electrons</i> definition</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Based_on_protons.2C_neutrons_and_electrons"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Based_on_protons,_neutrons_and_electrons">Based on protons, neutrons and electrons</span></h3>\n<p>A definition of "matter" more fine-scale than the atoms and molecules definition is: <i>matter is made up of what <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> are made of</i>, meaning anything made of positively charged <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a>, neutral <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a>, and negatively charged <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DePodesta1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-DePodesta1-12">[12]</a></sup> This definition goes beyond atoms and molecules, however, to include substances made from these building blocks that are <i>not</i> simply atoms or molecules, for example electron beams in an old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">cathode ray tube</a> television, or <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> matter&#8212;typically, carbon and oxygen nuclei in a sea of degenerate electrons. At a microscopic level, the constituent "particles" of matter such as protons, neutrons, and electrons obey the laws of quantum mechanics and exhibit wave&#8211;particle duality. At an even deeper level, protons and neutrons are made up of <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and the force fields (<a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a>) that bind them together, leading to the next definition.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Based_on_quarks_and_leptons">Based on quarks and leptons</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="316" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/330px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/495px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg/660px-Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standard_Model_of_Elementary_Particles.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Under the "quarks and leptons" definition, the elementary and composite particles made of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarks" title="Quarks">quarks</a> (in purple) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leptons" title="Leptons">leptons</a> (in green) would be matter&#8212;while the gauge bosons (in red) would not be matter. However, interaction energy inherent to composite particles (for example, gluons involved in neutrons and protons) contribute to the mass of ordinary matter.</div></div></div>\n<p>As seen in the above discussion, many early definitions of what can be called "ordinary matter" were based upon its structure or "building blocks".  On the scale of elementary particles, a definition that follows this tradition can be stated as:\n"ordinary matter is everything that is composed of <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>", or "ordinary matter is everything that is composed of any elementary fermions except antiquarks and antileptons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Povh0_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Povh0-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carithers_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carithers-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The connection between these formulations follows.\n</p><p>Leptons (the most famous being the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>), and quarks (of which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baryons" title="Baryons">baryons</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protons" title="Protons">protons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrons" title="Neutrons">neutrons</a>, are made) combine to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atoms" title="Atoms">atoms</a>, which in turn form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a>. Because atoms and molecules are said to be matter, it is natural to phrase the definition as: "ordinary matter is anything that is made of the same things that atoms and molecules are made of". (However, notice that one also can make from these building blocks matter that is <i>not</i> atoms or molecules.) Then, because electrons are leptons, and protons, and neutrons are made of quarks, this definition in turn leads to the definition of matter as being "quarks and leptons", which are two of the four types of elementary fermions (the other two being antiquarks and antileptons, which can be considered antimatter as described later). Carithers and Grannis state: "Ordinary matter is composed entirely of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Generation_(physics)" title="Generation (physics)">first-generation</a> particles, namely the [up] and [down] quarks, plus the electron and its neutrino."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carithers_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carithers-14">[14]</a></sup> (Higher generations particles quickly decay into first-generation particles, and thus are not commonly encountered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2005_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green2005-16">[16]</a></sup>)\n</p><p>This definition of ordinary matter is more subtle than it first appears. All the particles that make up ordinary matter (leptons and quarks) are elementary fermions, while all the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_carriers" title="Force carriers">force carriers</a> are elementary bosons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smolin_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smolin-17">[17]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/W_and_Z_bosons" title="W and Z bosons">W and Z bosons</a> that mediate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_force" title="Weak force">weak force</a> are not made of quarks or leptons, and so are not ordinary matter, even if they have mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caso_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caso-18">[18]</a></sup> In other words, <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> is not something that is exclusive to ordinary matter.\n</p><p>The quark&#8211;lepton definition of ordinary matter, however, identifies not only the elementary building blocks of matter, but also includes composites made from the constituents (atoms and molecules, for example). Such composites contain an interaction energy that holds the constituents together, and may constitute the bulk of the mass of the composite. As an example, to a great extent, the mass of an atom is simply the sum of the masses of its constituent protons, neutrons and electrons. However, digging deeper, the protons and neutrons are made up of quarks bound together by gluon fields (see <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics#Dynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">dynamics of quantum chromodynamics</a>) and these gluons fields contribute significantly to the mass of hadrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aitchison_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aitchison-19">[19]</a></sup> In other words, most of what composes the "mass" of ordinary matter is due to the <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> of quarks within protons and neutrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Povh_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Povh-20">[20]</a></sup> For example, the sum of the mass of the three quarks in a <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a> is approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001125000000000000&#9824;</span>12.5&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt#Mass" title="Electronvolt">MeV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></a></span>, which is low compared to the mass of a nucleon (approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002938000000000000&#9824;</span>938&#160;<a href="/wiki/Electronvolt#Mass" title="Electronvolt">MeV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></a></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green2004_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green2004-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Akai_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Akai-22">[22]</a></sup> The bottom line is that most of the mass of everyday objects comes from the interaction energy of its elementary components.\n</p><p>The Standard Model groups matter particles into three generations, where each generation consists of two quarks and two leptons. The first generation is the <i><a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down</a></i> quarks, the <i><a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrino</a></i>; the second includes the <i><a href="/wiki/Charm_quark" title="Charm quark">charm</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_quark" title="Strange quark">strange</a></i> quarks, the <i><a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muon</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Muon_neutrino" title="Muon neutrino">muon neutrino</a></i>; the third generation consists of the <i><a href="/wiki/Top_quark" title="Top quark">top</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Bottom_quark" title="Bottom quark">bottom</a></i> quarks and the <i><a href="/wiki/Tau_(particle)" title="Tau (particle)">tau</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tau_neutrino" title="Tau neutrino">tau neutrino</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Staley_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Staley-23">[23]</a></sup> The most natural explanation for this would be that quarks and leptons of higher generations are <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited states</a> of the first generations. If this turns out to be the case, it would imply that quarks and leptons are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_particle" title="Composite particle">composite particles</a>, rather than <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirsh_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirsh-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This quark&#8211;lepton definition of matter also leads to what can be described as "conservation of (net) matter" laws&#8212;discussed later below.  Alternatively, one could return to the mass&#8211;volume&#8211;space concept of matter, leading to the next definition, in which antimatter becomes included as a subclass of matter.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Based_on_elementary_fermions_.28mass.2C_volume.2C_and_space.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Based_on_elementary_fermions_(mass,_volume,_and_space)">Based on elementary fermions (mass, volume, and space)</span></h3>\n<p>A common or traditional definition of matter is "anything that has <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> (occupies <a href="/wiki/Space" title="Space">space</a>)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hage_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hage-26">[26]</a></sup> For example, a car would be said to be made of matter, as it has mass and volume (occupies space).\n</p><p>The observation that matter occupies space goes back to antiquity. However, an explanation for why matter occupies space is recent, and is argued to be a result of the phenomenon described in the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peacock_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peacock-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kreiger_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kreiger-28">[28]</a></sup> which applies to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermions" title="Fermions">fermions</a>.  Two particular examples where the exclusion principle clearly relates matter to the occupation of space are white dwarf stars and neutron stars, discussed further below.\n</p><p>Thus, matter can be defined as everything composed of elementary fermions. Although we don\'t encounter them in everyday life, antiquarks (such as the <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">antiproton</a>) and antileptons (such as the <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>) are the <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiparticles</a> of the quark and the lepton, are elementary fermions as well, and have essentially the same properties as quarks and leptons, including the applicability of the Pauli exclusion principle which can be said to prevent two particles from being in the same place at the same time (in the same state), i.e. makes each particle "take up space".  This particular definition leads to matter being defined to include anything made of these <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> particles as well as the ordinary quark and lepton, and thus also anything made of <a href="/wiki/Meson" title="Meson">mesons</a>, which are unstable particles made up of a quark and an antiquark.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_general_relativity_and_cosmology">In general relativity and cosmology</span></h3>\n<p>In the context of <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">relativity</a>, mass is not an additive quantity, in the sense that one can not add the rest masses of particles in a system to get the total rest mass of the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penrose_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Penrose-1">[1]</a></sup> Thus, in relativity usually a more general view is that it is not the sum of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest masses</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Stress%E2%80%93energy_tensor" title="Stress&#8211;energy tensor">energy&#8211;momentum tensor</a> that quantifies the amount of matter. This tensor gives the rest mass for the entire system. "Matter" therefore is sometimes considered as anything that contributes to the energy&#8211;momentum of a system, that is, anything that is not purely gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caroll_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caroll-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies499_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davies499-30">[30]</a></sup> This view is commonly held in fields that deal with <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>. In this view, light and other massless particles and fields are all part of "matter".\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>\n<p>In particle physics, fermions are particles that obey <a href="/wiki/Fermi%E2%80%93Dirac_statistics" title="Fermi&#8211;Dirac statistics">Fermi&#8211;Dirac statistics</a>. Fermions can be elementary, like the electron&#8212;or composite, like the proton and neutron. In the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a>, there are two types of elementary fermions: quarks and leptons, which are discussed next.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quarks">Quarks</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">Quark</a></div>\n<p>Quarks are particles of <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">spin-&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span></a>, implying that they are <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a>. They carry an <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> of &#8722;&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>3</sub></span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">e</a> (down-type quarks) or +&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>2</sup>&#8260;<sub>3</sub></span>&#160;e (up-type quarks). For comparison, an electron has a charge of &#8722;1&#160;e. They also carry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_charge" title="Colour charge">colour charge</a>, which is the equivalent of the electric charge for the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interaction</a>. Quarks also undergo <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>, meaning that they are subject to the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interaction</a>. Quarks are massive particles, and therefore are also subject to <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin:0 auto; atom\n</td>\n<td>antitop\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">t</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup protons\n</td>\n<td>antibottom\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proton_quark_structure.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Proton_quark_structure.svg/170px-Proton_quark_structure.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Proton_quark_structure.svg/255px-Proton_quark_structure.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Proton_quark_structure.svg/340px-Proton_quark_structure.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proton_quark_structure.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Quark structure of a proton: 2 up quarks and 1 down quark.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Baryonic_matter">Baryonic matter</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">Baryon</a></div>\n<p>Baryons are strongly interacting fermions, and so are subject to Fermi&#8211;Dirac statistics. Amongst the baryons are the protons and neutrons, which occur in atomic nuclei, but many other unstable baryons exist as well. The term <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryon</a> usually refers to triquarks&#8212;particles made of three quarks. Also, "exotic" baryons made of four quarks and one antiquark are known as <a href="/wiki/Pentaquark" title="Pentaquark">pentaquarks</a>, but their existence is not generally accepted.\n</p><p>Baryonic matter is the part of the universe that is made of baryons (including all atoms). This part of the universe does not include <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_holes" title="Black holes">black holes</a> or various forms of degenerate matter, such as compose <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> stars and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>. Microwave light seen by <a href="/wiki/Wilkinson_Microwave_Anisotropy_Probe" title="Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe">Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</a> (WMAP), suggests that only about 4.6% of that part of the universe within range of the best <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a> (that is, matter that may be visible because light could reach us from it), is made of baryonic matter. About 26.8% is dark matter, and about 68.3% is dark energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_Science_Dark_Matter_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Science_Dark_Matter-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As a matter of fact, the great majority of ordinary matter in the universe is unseen, since visible stars and gas inside galaxies and clusters account for less than 10 per cent of the ordinary matter contribution to the mass&#8211;energy density of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Size_IK_Peg.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="1051" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Size_IK_Peg.svg/220px-Size_IK_Peg.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Size_IK_Peg.svg/330px-Size_IK_Peg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Size_IK_Peg.svg/440px-Size_IK_Peg.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Size_IK_Peg.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison between the white dwarf <a href="/wiki/IK_Pegasi" title="IK Pegasi">IK Pegasi</a> B (center), its A-class companion IK Pegasi A (left) and the Sun (right). This white dwarf has a surface temperature of 35,500&#160;K.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hadronic_matter">Hadronic matter</span></h4>\n<p>Hadronic matter can refer to \'ordinary\' baryonic matter, made from <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">Baryons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meson" title="Meson">mesons</a>), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quark_matter" title="Quark matter">quark matter</a> (a generalisation  of atomic nuclei), ie. the \'low\' temperature <a href="/wiki/QCD_matter" title="QCD matter">QCD matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> It includes <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">degenerate matter</a> and the result of high energy heavy nuclei collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Distinct from <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Degenerate_matter">Degenerate matter</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">Degenerate matter</a></div>\n<p>In physics, <i>degenerate matter</i> refers to the ground state of a gas of fermions at a temperature near absolute zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldberg0_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldberg0-36">[36]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a> requires that only two fermions can occupy a quantum state, one spin-up and the other spin-down. Hence, at zero temperature, the fermions fill up sufficient levels to accommodate all the available fermions&#8212;and in the case of many fermions, the maximum kinetic energy (called the <i><a href="/wiki/Fermi_energy" title="Fermi energy">Fermi energy</a></i>) and the pressure of the gas becomes very large, and depends on the number of fermions rather than the temperature, unlike normal states of matter.\n</p><p>Degenerate matter is thought to occur during the evolution of heavy stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldberg1_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldberg1-37">[37]</a></sup> The demonstration by <a href="/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar" title="Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar">Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar</a> that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_dwarf_star" title="White dwarf star">white dwarf stars</a> have a maximum allowed mass because of the exclusion principle caused a revolution in the theory of star evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luminet_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luminet-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Degenerate matter includes the part of the universe that is made up of neutron stars and white dwarfs.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strange_matter">Strange matter</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Strange_matter" title="Strange matter">Strange matter</a></div>\n<p><i>Strange matter</i> is a particular form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quark_matter" title="Quark matter">quark matter</a>, usually thought of as a <i>liquid</i> of <a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up</a>, <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strange_quark" title="Strange quark">strange</a> <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>. It is contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_matter" title="Nuclear matter">nuclear matter</a>, which is a liquid of <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> (which themselves are built out of up and down quarks), and with non-strange quark matter, which is a quark liquid that contains only up and down quarks. At high enough density, strange matter is expected to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Color_superconductor" title="Color superconductor">color superconducting</a>. Strange matter is hypothesized to occur in the core of <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, or, more speculatively, as isolated droplets that may vary in size from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Femtometer" title="Femtometer">femtometers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Strangelet" title="Strangelet">strangelets</a>) to kilometers (<a href="/wiki/Quark_star" title="Quark star">quark stars</a>).\n</p>\n<h5><span id="Two_meanings_of_the_term_.22strange_matter.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id=\'Two_meanings_of_the_term_"strange_matter"\'>Two meanings of the term "strange matter"</span></h5>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>, the term is used in two ways, one broader and the other more specific.\n</p>\n<ol><li>The broader meaning is just quark matter that contains three flavors of quarks: up, down, and strange. In this definition, there is a critical pressure and an associated critical density, and when nuclear matter (made of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protons" title="Protons">protons</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrons" title="Neutrons">neutrons</a>) is compressed beyond this density, the protons and neutrons dissociate into quarks, yielding quark matter (probably strange matter).</li>\n<li>The narrower meaning is quark matter that is <i>more stable than nuclear matter</i>. The idea that this could happen is the "strange matter hypothesis" of Bodmer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and Witten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> In this definition, the critical pressure is zero: the true ground state of matter is <i>always</i> quark matter. The nuclei that we see in the matter around us, which are droplets of nuclear matter, are actually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastable" title="Metastable">metastable</a>, and given enough time (or the right external stimulus) would decay into droplets of strange matter, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Strangelet" title="Strangelet">strangelets</a>.</li></ol>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leptons">Leptons</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">Lepton</a></div>\n<p>Leptons are particles of <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">spin-&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span></a>, meaning that they are <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermions</a>. They carry an <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> of &#8722;1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">e</a> (charged leptons) or 0&#160;e (neutrinos). Unlike quarks, leptons do not carry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_charge" title="Colour charge">colour charge</a>, meaning that they do not experience the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interaction</a>. Leptons also undergo radioactive decay, meaning that they are subject to the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interaction</a>. Leptons are massive particles, therefore are subject to gravity.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin:0 auto; text-align:center;">\n<caption>Lepton
