style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Muon_neutrino" title="Muon neutrino">muon neutrino</a>\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#956;</sub></span></span>\n</td>\n<td>&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>\n</td>\n<td>0\n</td>\n<td>&lt; 0.19\n</td>\n<td>&lt; &#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> electron\n</td>\n<td>muon antineutrino\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#956;</sub></span></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Tau_neutrino" title="Tau neutrino">tau neutrino</a>\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>&#957;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#964;</sub></span></span>\n</td>\n<td>&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>\n</td>\n<td>0\n</td>\n<td>&lt; 18.2\n</td>\n<td>&lt; 40 electrons\n</td>\n<td>tau antineutrino\n</td>\n<td><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">&#957;</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#964;</sub></span></span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases">Phases</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">Phase (matter)</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">Phase diagram</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">State of matter</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="503" data-file-width="595" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG/280px-Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG/420px-Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG/560px-Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_for_pure_substance.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phase diagram for a typical substance at a fixed volume. Vertical axis is <i>P</i>ressure, horizontal axis is <i>T</i>emperature. The green line marks the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freezing_point" title="Freezing point">freezing point</a> (above the green line is <i>solid</i>, below it is <i>liquid</i>) and the blue line the <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> (above it is <i>liquid</i> and below it is <i>gas</i>). So, for example, at higher <i>T</i>, a higher <i>P</i> is necessary to maintain the substance in liquid phase. At the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> the three phases; liquid, gas and solid; can coexist. Above the <a href="/wiki/Critical_point_(thermodynamics)" title="Critical point (thermodynamics)">critical point</a> there is no detectable difference between the phases. The dotted line shows the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)#Density_of_water_and_ice" title="Water (molecule)">anomalous behavior of water</a>: ice melts at constant temperature with increasing pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Logan_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Logan-43">[43]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bulk" title="wikt:bulk">bulk</a>, matter can exist in several different forms, or states of aggregation, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phases</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collings_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collings-44">[44]</a></sup> depending on ambient <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trevena_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trevena-45">[45]</a></sup> A phase is a form of matter that has a relatively uniform chemical composition and physical properties (such as <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat" title="Specific heat">specific heat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a>, and so forth). These phases include the three familiar ones (<a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gases</a>), as well as more exotic states of matter (such as <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasmas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superfluid" title="Superfluid">superfluids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supersolid" title="Supersolid">supersolids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein condensate">Bose&#8211;Einstein condensates</a>, ...). A <i><a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a></i> may be a liquid, gas or plasma. There are also <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ferromagnetism" title="Ferromagnetism">ferromagnetic</a> phases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_material" title="Magnetic material">magnetic materials</a>. As conditions change, matter may change from one phase into another. These phenomena are called <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transitions</a>, and are studied in the field of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>. In nanomaterials, the vastly increased ratio of surface area to volume results in matter that can exhibit properties entirely different from those of bulk material, and not well described by any bulk phase (see <a href="/wiki/Nanomaterials" title="Nanomaterials">nanomaterials</a> for more details).\n</p><p>Phases are sometimes called <i>states of matter</i>, but this term can lead to confusion with <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamic states</a>. For example, two gases maintained at different pressures are in different <i>thermodynamic states</i> (different pressures), but in the same <i>phase</i> (both are gases).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antimatter">Antimatter</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">Antimatter</a></div>\n<table style="float:right; width:30%; margin:0em 1em 1em 1em; background:#eee; border:#ccc solid">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_dropshade.png"><img alt="Question dropshade.png" data-file-height="50" data-file-width="50" decoding="async" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/20px-Question_dropshade.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/30px-Question_dropshade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Question_dropshade.png/40px-Question_dropshade.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td>\n<td><b>Unsolved problem in physics</b>:<br/><div style="padding:5px; font-style:italic;"><a href="/wiki/Baryon_asymmetry" title="Baryon asymmetry">Baryon asymmetry</a>. Why is there far more matter than antimatter in the observable universe? </div><small><a href="/wiki/List_of_unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="List of unsolved problems in physics">(more unsolved problems in physics)</a></small>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chemistry" title="Quantum chemistry">quantum chemistry</a>, <i>antimatter</i> is matter that is composed of the <a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">antiparticles</a> of those that constitute ordinary matter. If a particle and its antiparticle come into contact with each other, the two <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilate</a>; that is, they may both be converted into other particles with equal <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> in accordance with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein" title="Einstein">Einstein</a>\'s equation <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E%3DMC2" title="E=MC2"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></a></span>. These new particles may be high-energy <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>) or other particle&#8211;antiparticle pairs. The resulting particles are endowed with an amount of kinetic energy equal to the difference between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">rest mass</a> of the products of the annihilation and the rest mass of the original particle&#8211;antiparticle pair, which is often quite large.  Depending on which definition of "matter" is adopted, antimatter can be said to be a particular subclass of matter, or the opposite of matter.\n</p><p>Antimatter is not found naturally on Earth, except very briefly and in vanishingly small quantities (as the result of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>). This is because antimatter that came to exist on Earth outside the confines of a suitable physics laboratory would almost instantly meet the ordinary matter that Earth is made of, and be annihilated. Antiparticles and some stable antimatter (such as <a href="/wiki/Antihydrogen" title="Antihydrogen">antihydrogen</a>) can be made in tiny amounts, but not in enough quantity to do more than test a few of its theoretical properties.\n</p><p>There is considerable speculation both in <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> as to why the observable universe is apparently almost entirely matter (in the sense of quarks and leptons but not antiquarks or antileptons), and whether other places are almost entirely antimatter (antiquarks and antileptons) instead. In the early universe, it is thought that matter and antimatter were equally represented, and the disappearance of antimatter requires an asymmetry in physical laws called <a href="/wiki/CP_violation" title="CP violation">CP (charge-parity) symmetry violation</a>, which can be obtained from the Standard Model,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CP_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-CP-46">[46]</a></sup> but at this time the apparent <a href="/wiki/Asymmetry" title="Asymmetry">asymmetry</a> of matter and antimatter in the visible universe is one of the great <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsolved_problems_in_physics" title="Unsolved problems in physics">unsolved problems in physics</a>. Possible processes by which it came about are explored in more detail under <a href="/wiki/Baryogenesis" title="Baryogenesis">baryogenesis</a>.\n</p><p>Formally, antimatter particles can be defined by their negative <a href="/wiki/Baryon_number" title="Baryon number">baryon number</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a>, while "normal" (non-antimatter) matter particles have positive baryon or lepton number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>  These two classes of particles are the antiparticle partners of one another.\n</p><p>In October 2017, scientists reported further evidence that matter and <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>, equally produced at the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, are identical, should completely annihilate each other and, as a result, the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> should not exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TT-20171025_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-TT-20171025-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20171020_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20171020-49">[49]</a></sup> This implies that there must be something, as yet unknown to scientists, that either stopped the complete mutual destruction of matter and antimatter in the early forming universe, or that gave rise to an imbalance between the two forms.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_of_matter">Conservation of matter</span></h2>\n<p>Two quantities that can define an amount of matter in the quark&#8211;lepton sense (and antimatter in an antiquark&#8211;antilepton sense), <a href="/wiki/Baryon_number" title="Baryon number">baryon number</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepton_number" title="Lepton number">lepton number</a>, are <a href="/wiki/Conservation_law" title="Conservation law">conserved</a> in the Standard Model. A <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryon</a> such as the proton or neutron has a baryon number of one, and a quark, because there are three in a baryon, is given a baryon number of 1/3.  So the net amount of matter, as measured by the number of quarks (minus the number of antiquarks, which each have a baryon number of &#8722;1/3), which is proportional to baryon number, and number of leptons (minus antileptons), which is called the lepton number, is practically impossible to change in any process.   Even in a nuclear bomb, none of the baryons (protons and neutrons of which the atomic nuclei are composed) are destroyed&#8212;there are as many baryons after as before the reaction, so none of these matter particles are actually destroyed and none are even converted to non-matter particles (like photons of light or radiation).  Instead, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_binding_energy" title="Nuclear binding energy">nuclear</a> (and perhaps <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics_binding_energy" title="Quantum chromodynamics binding energy">chromodynamic) binding energy</a> is released, as these baryons become bound into mid-size nuclei having less energy (and, <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">equivalently</a>, less <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_per_nucleon" title="Mass per nucleon">mass) per nucleon</a> compared to the original small (hydrogen) and large (plutonium etc.) nuclei.  Even in <a href="/wiki/Electron%E2%80%93positron_annihilation" title="Electron&#8211;positron annihilation">electron&#8211;positron annihilation</a>, there is no net matter being destroyed, because there was zero net matter (zero total lepton number and baryon number) to begin with before the annihilation&#8212;one lepton minus one antilepton equals zero net lepton number&#8212;and this net amount matter does not change as it simply remains zero after the annihilation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> So the only way to really "destroy" or "convert" ordinary matter is to pair it with the same amount of antimatter so that their "matterness" cancels out&#8212;but in practice there is almost no antimatter generally available in the universe (see <a href="/wiki/Baryon_asymmetry" title="Baryon asymmetry">baryon asymmetry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leptogenesis_(physics)" title="Leptogenesis (physics)">leptogenesis</a>) with which to do so.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_types">Other types</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matter_Distribution.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="618" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Matter_Distribution.JPG/280px-Matter_Distribution.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Matter_Distribution.JPG/420px-Matter_Distribution.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Matter_Distribution.JPG/560px-Matter_Distribution.JPG 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matter_Distribution.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pie chart showing the fractions of energy in the universe contributed by different sources. <i>Ordinary matter</i> is divided into <i>luminous matter</i> (the stars and luminous gases and 0.005% radiation) and <i>nonluminous matter</i> (intergalactic gas and about 0.1% neutrinos and 0.04% supermassive black holes). Ordinary matter is uncommon. Modeled after Ostriker and Steinhardt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostriker_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostriker-51">[51]</a></sup> For more information, see <a class="external text" href="http://map.gsfc.nasa.gov/news/index.html" rel="nofollow">NASA</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Ordinary matter, in the quarks and leptons definition, constitutes about 4% of the <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass&#8211;energy equivalence">energy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a>. The remaining energy is theorized to be due to exotic forms, of which 23% is <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pretzl_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pretzl-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-53">[53]</a></sup> and 73% is <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">dark energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler0_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler0-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gribbin_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gribbin-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rotation_curve_(Milky_Way).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="419" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG/280px-Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG/420px-Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG/560px-Rotation_curve_%28Milky_Way%29.JPG 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rotation_curve_(Milky_Way).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">Galaxy rotation curve</a> for the Milky Way. Vertical axis is speed of rotation about the galactic center. Horizontal axis is distance from the galactic center. The sun is marked with a yellow ball. The observed curve of speed of rotation is blue. The predicted curve based upon stellar mass and gas in the Milky Way is red. The difference is due to <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> or perhaps a modification of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOND" title="MOND">law of gravity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koupelis_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koupelis-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-58">[58]</a></sup> Scatter in observations is indicated roughly by gray bars.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_matter">Dark matter</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">Dark matter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lambda-CDM_model" title="Lambda-CDM model">Lambda-CDM model</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WIMPs" title="WIMPs">WIMPs</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">Galaxy formation and evolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter_halo" title="Dark matter halo">Dark matter halo</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, <i>dark matter</i> is matter of unknown composition that does not emit or reflect enough electromagnetic radiation to be observed directly, but whose presence can be inferred from gravitational effects on visible matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majumdar_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Majumdar-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olive_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olive-60">[60]</a></sup> Observational evidence of the early universe and the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory require that this matter have energy and mass, but is not composed ordinary baryons (protons and neutrons). The commonly accepted view is that most of the dark matter is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonbaryonic_dark_matter" title="Nonbaryonic dark matter">non-baryonic in nature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majumdar_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Majumdar-59">[59]</a></sup> As such, it is composed of particles as yet unobserved in the laboratory. Perhaps they are <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">supersymmetric particles</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olive2_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olive2-61">[61]</a></sup> which are not <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> particles, but relics formed at very high energies in the early phase of the universe and still floating about.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Majumdar_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Majumdar-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dark_energy">Dark energy</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang#Dark_energy" title="Big Bang">Big Bang &#167;&#160;Dark energy</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, <i>dark energy</i> is the name given to source of the repelling influence that is accelerating the rate of <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">expansion of the universe</a>. Its precise nature is currently a mystery, although its effects can reasonably be modeled by assigning matter-like properties such as energy density and pressure to the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler1_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler1-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smolin2_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smolin2-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Fully 70% of the matter density in the universe appears to be in the form of dark energy. Twenty-six percent is dark matter. Only 4% is ordinary matter. So less than 1 part in 20 is made out of matter we have observed experimentally or described in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_model" title="Standard model">standard model</a> of particle physics. Of the other 96%, apart from the properties just mentioned, we know absolutely nothing.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Lee_Smolin" title="Lee Smolin">Lee Smolin</a>: <i>The Trouble with Physics</i>, p. 16</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exotic_matter">Exotic matter</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exotic_matter" title="Exotic matter">Exotic matter</a></div>\n<p>Exotic matter is a concept of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, which may include dark matter and dark energy but goes further to include any hypothetical material that violates one or more of the properties of known forms of matter. Some such materials might possess hypothetical properties like <a href="/wiki/Negative_mass" title="Negative mass">negative mass</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_development">Historical development</span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Antiquity_.28c._610_BC.E2.80.93c._322_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity_(c._610_BC&#8211;c._322_BC)">Antiquity (c. 610 BC&#8211;c. 322 BC)</span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">pre-Socratics</a> were among the first recorded speculators about the underlying nature of the visible world. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a> (c. 624 BC&#8211;c. 546 BC) regarded water as the fundamental material of the world. <a href="/wiki/Anaximander" title="Anaximander">Anaximander</a> (c. 610 BC&#8211;c. 546 BC) posited that the basic material was wholly characterless or limitless: the Infinite (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apeiron_(cosmology)" title="Apeiron (cosmology)">apeiron</a></i>). <a href="/wiki/Anaximenes_of_Miletus" title="Anaximenes of Miletus">Anaximenes</a> (flourished 585 BC, d. 528 BC) posited that the basic stuff was <i>pneuma</i> or air. <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a> (c. 535&#8211;c. 475 BC) seems to say the basic element is fire, though perhaps he means that all is change. <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> (c. 490&#8211;430 BC) spoke of four <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">elements</a> of which everything was made: earth, water, air, and fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> argued that change does not exist, and <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> argued that everything is composed of minuscule, inert bodies of all shapes called atoms, a philosophy called <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a>. All of these notions had deep philosophical problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384&#8211;322 BC) was the first to put the conception on a sound philosophical basis, which he did in his natural philosophy, especially in <a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)"><i>Physics</i></a> book I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> He adopted as reasonable suppositions the four <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">Empedoclean elements</a>, but added a fifth, <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">aether</a>. Nevertheless, these elements are not basic in Aristotle\'s mind. Rather they, like everything else in the visible world, are composed of the basic <i>principles</i> matter and form.\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For my definition of matter is just this&#8212;the primary substratum of each thing, from which it comes to be without qualification, and which persists in the result.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>Aristotle, Physics I:9:192a32</cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The word Aristotle uses for matter, <a href="/wiki/Hyle" title="Hyle">&#8021;&#955;&#951; (<i>hyle</i> or <i>hule</i>)</a>, can be literally translated as wood or timber, that is, "raw material" for building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Indeed, Aristotle\'s conception of matter is intrinsically linked to something being made or composed. In other words, in contrast to the early modern conception of matter as simply occupying space, matter for Aristotle is definitionally linked to process or change: matter is what underlies a change of substance. For example, a horse eats grass: the horse changes the grass into itself; the grass as such does not persist in the horse, but some aspect of it&#8212;its matter&#8212;does. The matter is not specifically described (e.g., as <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>), but consists of whatever persists in the change of substance from grass to horse. Matter in this understanding does not exist independently (i.e., as a <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>), but exists interdependently (i.e., as a "principle") with form and only insofar as it underlies change. It can be helpful to conceive of the relationship of matter and form as very similar to that between parts and whole. For Aristotle, matter as such can only <i>receive</i> actuality from form; it has no activity or actuality in itself, similar to the way that parts as such only have their existence <i>in</i> a whole (otherwise they would be independent wholes).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seventeenth_and_eighteenth_centuries">Seventeenth and eighteenth centuries</span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren&#233; Descartes">Ren&#233; Descartes</a> (1596&#8211;1650) originated the modern conception of matter. He was primarily a geometer. Instead of, like Aristotle, deducing the existence of matter from the physical reality of change, Descartes arbitrarily postulated matter to be an abstract, mathematical substance that occupies space:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>So, extension in length, breadth, and depth, constitutes the nature of bodily substance; and thought constitutes the nature of thinking substance. And everything else attributable to body presupposes extension, and is only a mode of extended</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>Ren&#233; Descartes, Principles of Philosophy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>For Descartes, matter has only the property of extension, so its only activity aside from locomotion is to exclude other bodies:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> this is the <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_(philosophy)" title="Mechanism (philosophy)">mechanical philosophy</a>. Descartes makes an absolute distinction between mind, which he defines as unextended, thinking substance, and matter, which he defines as unthinking, extended substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> They are independent things. In contrast, Aristotle defines matter and the formal/forming principle as complementary <i>principles</i> that together compose one independent thing (<a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a>). In short, Aristotle defines matter (roughly speaking) as what things are actually made of (with a <i>potential</i> independent existence), but Descartes elevates matter to an actual independent thing in itself.\n</p><p>The continuity and difference between Descartes\' and Aristotle\'s conceptions is noteworthy. In both conceptions, matter is passive or inert. In the respective conceptions matter has different relationships to intelligence. For Aristotle, matter and intelligence (form) exist together in an interdependent relationship, whereas for Descartes, matter and intelligence (mind) are definitionally opposed, independent <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substances</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Descartes\' justification for restricting the inherent qualities of matter to extension is its permanence, but his real criterion is not permanence (which equally applied to color and resistance), but his desire to use geometry to explain all material properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Like Descartes, Hobbes, Boyle, and Locke argued that the inherent properties of bodies were limited to extension, and that so-called secondary qualities, like color, were only products of human perception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1643&#8211;1727) inherited Descartes\' mechanical conception of matter. In the third of his "Rules of Reasoning in Philosophy", Newton lists the universal qualities of matter as "extension, hardness, impenetrability, mobility, and inertia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Similarly in <i>Optics</i> he conjectures that God created matter as "solid, massy, hard, impenetrable, movable particles", which were "...even so very hard as never to wear or break in pieces".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The "primary" properties of matter were amenable to mathematical description, unlike "secondary" qualities such as color or taste. Like Descartes, Newton rejected the essential nature of secondary qualities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Newton developed Descartes\' notion of matter by restoring to matter intrinsic properties in addition to extension (at least on a limited basis), such as mass. Newton\'s use of gravitational force, which worked "at a distance", effectively repudiated Descartes\' mechanics, in which interactions happened exclusively by contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chomsky_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chomsky-77">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though Newton\'s gravity would seem to be a <i>power</i> of bodies, Newton himself did not admit it to be an <i>essential</i> property of matter. Carrying the logic forward more consistently, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> (1733&#8211;1804) argued that corporeal properties transcend contact mechanics: chemical properties require the <i>capacity</i> for attraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chomsky_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chomsky-77">[77]</a></sup> He argued matter has other inherent powers besides the so-called primary qualities of Descartes, et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nineteenth_and_twentieth_centuries">Nineteenth and twentieth centuries</span></h3>\n<p>Since Priestley\'s time, there has been a massive expansion in knowledge of the constituents of the material world (viz., molecules, atoms, subatomic particles), but there has been no further development in the <i>definition</i> of matter. Rather the question has been set aside. <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a> (born 1928) summarizes the situation that has prevailed since that time:\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>What is the concept of body that finally emerged?[...] The answer is that there is no clear and definite conception of body.[...] Rather, the material world is whatever we discover it to be, with whatever properties it must be assumed to have for the purposes of explanatory theory. Any intelligible theory that offers genuine explanations and that can be assimilated to the core notions of physics becomes part of the theory of the material world, part of our account of body. If we have such a theory in some domain, we seek to assimilate it to the core notions of physics, perhaps modifying these notions as we carry out this enterprise. </p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite>Noam Chomsky, <i>Language and problems of knowledge: the Managua lectures</i>, p. 144<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chomsky_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chomsky-77">[77]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>So matter is whatever physics studies and the object of study of physics is matter: there is no independent general definition of matter, apart from its fitting into the methodology of measurement and controlled experimentation. In sum, the boundaries between what constitutes matter and everything else remains as vague as the <a href="/wiki/Demarcation_problem" title="Demarcation problem">demarcation problem</a> of delimiting science from everything else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 19th century, following the development of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, and of <a href="/wiki/Atomic_theory" title="Atomic theory">atomic theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a> were seen as being the fundamental constituents of matter; atoms formed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compound_(chemistry)" title="Compound (chemistry)">compounds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wenham_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wenham-80">[80]</a></sup><span id="note"></span>\n</p><p>The common definition in terms of occupying space and having mass is in contrast with most physical and chemical definitions of matter, which rely instead upon its structure and upon attributes not necessarily related to volume and mass. At the turn of the nineteenth century, the knowledge of matter began a rapid evolution.\n</p><p>Aspects of the Newtonian view still held sway. <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> discussed matter in his work <i>Matter and Motion</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell-81">[81]</a></sup> He carefully separates "matter" from space and time, and defines it in terms of the object referred to in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_first_law_of_motion" title="Newton\'s first law of motion">Newton\'s first law of motion</a>.\n</p><p>\nHowever, the Newtonian picture was not the whole story. In the 19th century, the term "matter" was actively discussed by a host of scientists and philosophers, and a brief outline can be found in Levere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levere_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levere-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (March 2011)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A textbook discussion from 1870 suggests matter is what is made up of atoms:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barker_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barker-83">[83]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>Three divisions of matter are recognized in science: masses, molecules and atoms. <br/>A Mass of matter is any portion of matter appreciable by the senses. <br/>A Molecule is the smallest particle of matter into which a body can be divided without losing its identity. <br/>An Atom is a still smaller particle produced by division of a molecule. </p></blockquote>\n<p>Rather than simply having the attributes of mass and occupying space, matter was held to have chemical and electrical properties. In 1909 the famous physicist <a href="/wiki/J._J._Thomson" title="J. J. Thomson">J. J. Thomson</a> (1856&#8211;1940) wrote about the "constitution of matter" and was concerned with the possible connection between matter and electrical charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomson_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomson-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is an entire literature concerning the "structure of matter", ranging from the "electrical structure" in the early 20th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson-85">[85]</a></sup> to the more recent "quark structure of matter", introduced today with the remark: <i>Understanding the quark structure of matter has been one of the most important advances in contemporary physics.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacob_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacob-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (March 2011)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In this connection, physicists speak of <i>matter fields</i>, and speak of particles as "quantum excitations of a mode of the matter field".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davies_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davies-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weinberg_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weinberg-9">[9]</a></sup> And here is a quote from de Sabbata and Gasperini: "With the word "matter" we denote, in this context, the sources of the interactions, that is <a href="/wiki/Spinor_field" title="Spinor field">spinor fields</a> (like <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>), which are believed to be the fundamental components of matter, or <a href="/wiki/Bosonic_field" title="Bosonic field">scalar fields</a>, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higgs_particle" title="Higgs particle">Higgs particles</a>, which are used to introduced mass in a <a href="/wiki/Gauge_theory" title="Gauge theory">gauge theory</a> (and that, however, could be composed of more fundamental fermion fields)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabbata_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sabbata-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (March 2011)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In the late 19th century with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomson_experiment" title="Thomson experiment">discovery</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, and in the early 20th century, with the <a href="/wiki/Geiger%E2%80%93Marsden_experiment" title="Geiger&#8211;Marsden experiment">discovery</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>, and the birth of <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">particle physics</a>, matter was seen as made up of electrons, <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> interacting to form atoms. Today, we know that even protons and neutrons are not indivisible, they can be divided into <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>, while electrons are part of a particle family called <a href="/wiki/Lepton" title="Lepton">leptons</a>. Both <a href="#Quarks_and_leptons_definition">quarks and leptons</a> are <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>, and are currently seen as being the fundamental constituents of matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Povh1_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Povh1-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These quarks and leptons interact through four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_forces" title="Fundamental forces">fundamental forces</a>: <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interactions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interactions</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle physics is currently the best explanation for all of physics, but despite decades of efforts, gravity cannot yet be accounted for at the quantum level; it is only described by <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">graviton</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allday2_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allday2-89">[89]</a></sup> Interactions between quarks and leptons are the result of an exchange of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_carriers" title="Force carriers">force-carrying particles</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>) between quarks and leptons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The force-carrying particles are not themselves building blocks. As one consequence, mass and energy (which cannot be created or destroyed) cannot always be related to matter (which can be created out of non-matter particles such as photons, or even out of pure energy, such as kinetic energy). Force carriers are usually not considered matter: the carriers of the electric force (photons) possess energy (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck_relation" title="Planck relation">Planck relation</a>) and the carriers of the weak force (<a href="/wiki/W_and_Z_bosons" title="W and Z bosons">W and Z bosons</a>) are massive, but neither are considered matter either.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhotonsMatter_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhotonsMatter-91">[91]</a></sup> However, while these particles are not considered matter, they do contribute to the total mass of atoms, <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particles</a>, and all systems that contain them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tipler0_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tipler0-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spitzer_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spitzer-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Summary">Summary</span></h2>\n<p>The modern conception of matter has been refined many times in history, in light of the improvement in knowledge of just <i>what</i> the basic building blocks are, and in how they interact.\nThe term "matter" is used throughout physics in a bewildering variety of contexts: for example, one refers to "<a href="/wiki/Condensed_matter_physics" title="Condensed matter physics">condensed matter physics</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaikin_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaikin-94">[94]</a></sup> "elementary matter",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greiner_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greiner-95">[95]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Parton_(particle_physics)" title="Parton (particle physics)">partonic</a>" matter, "<a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark</a>" matter, "<a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">anti</a>"-matter, "<a href="/wiki/Strange_matter" title="Strange matter">strange</a>" matter, and "<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_matter" title="Nuclear matter">nuclear</a>" matter. In discussions of matter and <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>, normal matter has been referred to by <a href="/wiki/Hannes_Alfv%C3%A9n" title="Hannes Alfv&#233;n">Alfv&#233;n</a> as <i>koinomatter</i> (Gk. <i>common matter</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> It is fair to say that in <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, there is no broad consensus as to a general definition of matter, and the term "matter" usually is used in conjunction with a specifying modifier.\n</p><p>The history of the concept of matter is a history of the fundamental <i>length scales</i> used to define matter. Different building blocks apply depending upon whether one defines matter on an atomic or elementary particle level. One may use a definition that matter is atoms, or that matter is <a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadrons</a>, or that matter is leptons and quarks depending upon the scale at which one wishes to define matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p>These quarks and leptons interact through four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_forces" title="Fundamental forces">fundamental forces</a>: <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interactions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong interactions</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> of particle physics is currently the best explanation for all of physics, but despite decades of efforts, gravity cannot yet be accounted for at the quantum level; it is only described by <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">classical physics</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">quantum gravity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graviton" title="Graviton">graviton</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allday2_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allday2-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div>\n<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;"><tbody><tr>\n<td style="width: 25%;text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">\n<p><b>Antimatter</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambiplasma" title="Ambiplasma">Ambiplasma</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antihydrogen" title="Antihydrogen">Antihydrogen</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antiparticle" title="Antiparticle">Antiparticle</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Particle_accelerator" title="Particle accelerator">Particle accelerator</a></li></ul>\n<p><b>Cosmology</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">Cosmological constant</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations" title="Friedmann equations">Friedmann equations</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_ontology" title="Physical ontology">Physical ontology</a></li></ul>\n<p>\n</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width: 25%;text-align: left; "What Is a Matter Particle?". <i>International Journal of Modern Physics E</i>. <b>15</b> (1): 259&#8211;272. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2006IJMPE..15..259C" rel="nofollow">2006IJMPE..15..259C</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1142%2FS0218301306003916" rel="nofollow">10.1142/S0218301306003916</a>. <q><i>(From Abstract:)</i> Positive baryon numbers (A&gt;0) and positive lepton numbers (L&gt;0) characterize matter particles while negative baryon numbers and negative lepton numbers characterize antimatter particles. Matter particles and antimatter particles belong to two distinct classes of particles. Matter neutral particles are particles characterized by both zero baryon number and zero lepton number. This third class of particles includes mesons formed by a quark and an antiquark pair (a pair of matter particle and antimatter particle) and bosons which are messengers of known interactions (photons for electromagnetism, W and Z bosons for the weak interaction, gluons for the strong interaction). The antiparticle of a matter particle belongs to the class of antimatter particles, the antiparticle of an antimatter particle belongs to the class of matter particles.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=International+Journal+of+Modern+Physics+E&amp;rft.atitle=What+Is+a+Matter+Particle%3F&amp;rft.volume=15&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=259-272&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1142%2FS0218301306003916&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2006IJMPE..15..259C&amp;rft.aulast=Tsan&amp;rft.aufirst=Ung+Chan&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMatter"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Green2005-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Green2005_16-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">\n<cite class="citation book">D.
